  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'

<span id="freeTextContainer17906067710016294824">Camille is doing her best to disappear. She barely eats, works at night as a cleaner and lives in a tiny attic room. Downstairs in a beautiful, ornate apartment, lives Philibert Marquet de la Durbellière, a shy, erudite, upper-class man with an unlikely flatmate in the shape of the foul-mouthed but talented chef, Franck. One freezing evening Philibert overcomes his excruci</span>
  <span id="freeText17906067710016294824" style="display:none">Camille is doing her best to disappear. She barely eats, works at night as a cleaner and lives in a tiny attic room. Downstairs in a beautiful, ornate apartment, lives Philibert Marquet de la Durbellière, a shy, erudite, upper-class man with an unlikely flatmate in the shape of the foul-mouthed but talented chef, Franck. One freezing evening Philibert overcomes his excruciating reticence to rescue Camille, unconscious, from her garret and bring her into his home.<br /><br />As she recovers Camille learns more about Philibert; about Franck and his guilt for his beloved but fragile grandmother Paulette, who is all he has left in the world; and about herself. And slowly, this curious quartet of misfits all discover the importance of food, friendship and love.</span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Both of these books are excellent literary works. I liked Hunting and Gathering better because of its depth and also because the characters were so ap<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Both of these books are excellent literary works. I liked Hunting and Gathering better because of its depth and also because the characters were so appealing. There's a film version as well, which is fairly true to the book and which I enjoyed.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer528158542" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer18277443192454620160">Despite 1) several years spent in Indiana, 2) an unstinted enjoyment of meat, and 3) the fact that I at one time had a mustache (it was the 80’s), I am <i>
  <b>not</b>
</i> a Ron Swanson replica. To prove this point, I read a book set in Europe. . . France, no less. And it didn’t even have one of those page-turning plots to drive it.<br/><br/>What it <i>did</i> have was a plenitude of French themes. Food, especially the fine dining variety, was foremost among them. Art was another. Cultural divides of the type pitting aristo</span>
  <span id="freeText18277443192454620160" style="display:none">Despite 1) several years spent in Indiana, 2) an unstinted enjoyment of meat, and 3) the fact that I at one time had a mustache (it was the 80’s), I am <i>
  <b>not</b>
</i> a Ron Swanson replica. To prove this point, I read a book set in Europe. . . France, no less. And it didn’t even have one of those page-turning plots to drive it.<br /><br />What it <i>did</i> have was a plenitude of French themes. Food, especially the fine dining variety, was foremost among them. Art was another. Cultural divides of the type pitting aristocrats against republicans also featured. Romance was another theme, but not in an entirely typical way. We all know about love triangles. Well, this was more of an appreciation tetrahedron, where the sides denoting closeness among the four main characters would vary in length at any given time. It seemed like the point of the book and the pleasure to be derived from it was in watching the tetrahedron change shape.<br /><br />The points defining this geometric object were Camille (a cleaning lady or really more of a starving artist – starving from anorexia, that is), Philibert (gentle, awkward, and to the manor born), Franck (a talented chef but one with attitude – brusque, tired, and hurting), and Franck’s grandmother (less self-sufficient than she’d like to be, good-hearted but not always able to give more than she takes). I wish that the “land of the misfit toys” reference wasn’t so popular so that I could claim it as my own and apply it here. Anyway, these four interact in the ways real people do. It takes time and intermittent acts of kindness for the bonds to form, and it proceeds in fits and starts.<br /><br />This is a quick read with plenty of dialog and a respect for the show-don’t-tell approach to character development. It’s strong on lifestyle, too. The author even sprinkles in a bit of philosophy, though nothing heavy. All in all it was a nice, light parfait; easy to consume even if not an omnivore’s fill.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18277443192454620160" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27203792" />
    <a title="Leah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/702812-leah"><img alt="Leah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1198185868p2/702812.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/27203792?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leah" href="/user/show/702812-leah">Leah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Leah by: </span>
      Jamie
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/702812-leah?shelf=general-fiction">general-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/702812-leah?shelf=heartbreaking">heartbreaking</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27203792" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1360772707491130067">This book was recommended to me by Jamie, and it was a perfect rec, thank you so much, lady.<br/><br/>Translated from French to English, Hunting and Gathering is a character - rather than plot - driven, slice of life portrait. Somewhere within these pages, the main character finds herself at a bookstore, pouring over a collection of the French cartoonist Sempe's drawings (as I best know for his work in The New Yorker.) As I read further, I began to realize: There it is. This book is like a Sempe drawing. </span>
  <span id="freeText1360772707491130067" style="display:none">This book was recommended to me by Jamie, and it was a perfect rec, thank you so much, lady.<br /><br />Translated from French to English, Hunting and Gathering is a character - rather than plot - driven, slice of life portrait. Somewhere within these pages, the main character finds herself at a bookstore, pouring over a collection of the French cartoonist Sempe's drawings (as I best know for his work in The New Yorker.) As I read further, I began to realize: There it is. This book is like a Sempe drawing. It is graceful and gentle and detailed, despite looking right into the heart of something that would otherwise revolt or depress. Rather, Gavalda makes it seem as though the beauty in these characters is actually due TO their dejection. I appreciate this, because it really is our muli-dimensional nature that makes us human.<br /><br />I admired Gavalda's approach in writing truth for her characters without ever showing judgment as an author- not even in the most subtle and unintentional way. This isn't easy, and takes trust on the author's part to just allow these personalities to unfold, even if they are sometimes ugly sides to show.<br /><br />As a reward for her discipline, her characters really come to life, flaws and all, and allow the reader to develop a relationship with them as if they really are "friends", as the characters themselves grow to know and love one another. It is how we are in life: we understand each other as complex beings and recognize that this is what draws us together. Shared pain of past experiences, shared joy and frustration and kindnesses. <br /><br />I fear that through this review, I could be making this story seem way too darling, when it is far from that sort of tone. I guess that is that je ne sais quoi (ha) that comes from balancing sentiment with the reality of burden, as well as the candid sharp wit of dialogue, both between characters and in their internal conversation. The perspective is balanced, and never takes you too far to one side or another without bringing you right back. Such is life! <br /><br />This book is now well dog-eared with pages I want to revisit, and I never do that. I guess maybe at the bottom of it all I read this book at just the right time. It was a great reminder that we is who we is, and everyone's just trying to do the best they can in this crazy world, while finding a little joy and connection to get through. I needed that. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1360772707491130067" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18153998294608128440">This will sound weird, but it's true: some books don't need plots. <br/>Hunting and Gathering is a perfect example of a character-driven novel. Nothing really monumental or dramatic happens, and instead the story is supported entirely by the four main characters. They are: Camille, a cleaning lady (or "cleaning operative") who lives in a tiny apartment by herself and doesn't eat; Philibert, an aristocrat lacking social skills who lives in Camille's building; his roommate Franck, a temperamental but t</span>
  <span id="freeText18153998294608128440" style="display:none">This will sound weird, but it's true: some books don't need plots. <br />Hunting and Gathering is a perfect example of a character-driven novel. Nothing really monumental or dramatic happens, and instead the story is supported entirely by the four main characters. They are: Camille, a cleaning lady (or "cleaning operative") who lives in a tiny apartment by herself and doesn't eat; Philibert, an aristocrat lacking social skills who lives in Camille's building; his roommate Franck, a temperamental but talented chef; and Franck's grandmother, Paulette, an old woman terrified of being placed in a nursing home. <br />Gavalda's writing style is simple and elegant, and her dialogue is amazingly well done. The ending of Hunting and Gathering is a little bit too well-wrapped up, but I didn't mind. All in all, a lovely story.</span>

<div id="review_24124468" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/24124468" />
    <a title="Kathleen Dixon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/716890-kathleen-dixon"><img alt="Kathleen Dixon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1387050331p2/716890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/24124468?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathleen Dixon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathleen Dixon" href="/user/show/716890-kathleen-dixon">Kathleen Dixon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Kathleen by: </span>
      Reading Seals
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer24124468" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1945173017896855122">It's just possible that I couldn't put this book down because I'm into avoidance tactics at the moment (avoiding the study I need to do for my 4 exams coming up in 2 weeks time), but, nevertheless, I couldn't put it down. I just loved these poor, sad people and their wonderful specialities and their struggling to find some normal type of existence. The way Gavalda brings them together is very clever, and the way she takes the flaws in their characters and weaves in a realisation about friendship</span>
  <span id="freeText1945173017896855122" style="display:none">It's just possible that I couldn't put this book down because I'm into avoidance tactics at the moment (avoiding the study I need to do for my 4 exams coming up in 2 weeks time), but, nevertheless, I couldn't put it down. I just loved these poor, sad people and their wonderful specialities and their struggling to find some normal type of existence. The way Gavalda brings them together is very clever, and the way she takes the flaws in their characters and weaves in a realisation about friendship and belonging, is delightful.<br /><br />I love the descriptions of Camille's art as so much more than skill and technique. And the transformation of the angry young Franck into an inspired chef each time he cooks (or thinks of cooking) is fabulous. And then there's Philibert, the stuttering, caught-in-the-past 'rescuer' who learns how to be not-scared. Finally, there's Paulette and her journey into dementia that is so poignantly told and interconnected with our other three.<br /><br />I'm going to read this book again.<br /><br />Oh yes - a friend read the book and then saw the movie. She found the movie disappointing - "sweet," she said, "but..."). I had recently seen a poster for the movie and decided on that that I wouldn't go to it. I liked Audrey Tautou in Amelie, but she doesn't look like I imagined Camille, and I couldn't figure which of the two men in the poster was Franck and which was Philibert. In the book there is no mistaking them.<br /><br />My decision not to see the movie has been confirmed by my friend's comments. My friend said that she asked her husband afterwards, and he hadn't realised that Camille suffered anorexia, nor that she was a sublimely gifted artist. Neither of these two highly necessary parts of the book development came out in the film. And, of course, no movie can have even half of the 'other' parts of the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1945173017896855122" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie" href="/user/show/217328-jamie">Jamie</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Leah McKenzie</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jamie by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/811230-molly">Molly</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27636407" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4874793146818091651">This book. <br/><br/>This book, this book, this book.<br/><br/>*Sigh*<br/><br/>It is lyrical in that way that only books translated from French can be. I rarely underline or highlight while reading fiction, but this book gave my pen a work out. It was just lovely. <br/><br/>The characters were complex and well developed and sweet. They were just so sweet. <br/><br/>It wasn't perfect, but it was just the thing. It was flawed, but still one of the best I'll read this year. <br/><br/>The biggest flaw for me was probably Philou's character. I don't like that</span>
  <span id="freeText4874793146818091651" style="display:none">This book. <br /><br />This book, this book, this book.<br /><br />*Sigh*<br /><br />It is lyrical in that way that only books translated from French can be. I rarely underline or highlight while reading fiction, but this book gave my pen a work out. It was just lovely. <br /><br />The characters were complex and well developed and sweet. They were just so sweet. <br /><br />It wasn't perfect, but it was just the thing. It was flawed, but still one of the best I'll read this year. <br /><br />The biggest flaw for me was probably Philou's character. I don't like that he faded as Franck's character took over taking care of Camille. <br /><br />Other than that? Brill. And I won't lie, I finished it on the bus last night and cried.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4874793146818091651" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_383145253" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/383145253" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/383145253?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to El by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2635637-jenniferd">JenniferD</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=21st-centurylit">21st-centurylit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=artsy-fartsy">artsy-fartsy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer383145253" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18170297077499142927">I'm not great at reading contemporary novels for some reason. I think in a lot of ways I expect too much out of them, especially when I compare them to so many of their predecessors (totally unfair of me, I know, but that's how I roll). I usually see so much room for improvement, or "had this been tweaked <i>just a bit</i>..."'s, that I wind up wondering what everyone makes such a fuss about.<br/><br/>I don't wonder as much with this book. It's charming. It's heartbreaking in parts. It actually punched me in the</span>
  <span id="freeText18170297077499142927" style="display:none">I'm not great at reading contemporary novels for some reason. I think in a lot of ways I expect too much out of them, especially when I compare them to so many of their predecessors (totally unfair of me, I know, but that's how I roll). I usually see so much room for improvement, or "had this been tweaked <i>just a bit</i>..."'s, that I wind up wondering what everyone makes such a fuss about.<br /><br />I don't wonder as much with this book. It's charming. It's heartbreaking in parts. It actually punched me in the heart a few times. You know how certain paragraphs in books can hit you somewhere inside you didn't even know existed? Yeah, that's what happened with this book.<br /><br />There are four main characters - the starving artist, Camille; Philibert the aristocrat who lives in Camille's building; Philibert's roommate, Franck; and Paulette, Franck's grandmother. While the story shouldn't be told without any one of them, the only one I particularly cared about was Camille. Maybe it's because we're both of the womenfolk genre, and I could connect with her on that sort of level; but honestly I think it has more to do with how Gavalda wrote her. Camille is wonderful. I love the way she sees the world, and the way her art is an extension of her character. Everyone else in the story paled in comparison. I just wanted Camille.<br /><br />Now this is where I get obnoxious: I see that 10 Goodreads members have shelved this book as "chick lit". I'm not attacking them for that, but it just doesn't fit the bill in my opinion. This might be because my own definition of "chick lit" is less forgiving than I think it is according to most of the readers of the world. "Chick lit" to me is fluffy - that's what I imagine when I think of a baby chick. Fuzz. This was deeper than that. This wasn't a fuzzy story. There are the occasional fuzzy elements, but Camille is not a chick. She's not fluffy. She's bones and grit and paint. She's not out to find herself a man, or even the right pair of high heels. I'm not sure she would know how to walk in high heels. (Something else she and I have in common.) Most characters in what I define as "chick lit" do not appreciate the beauty in an ugly knitted scarf. Camille could.<br /><br />So I'm interested with that shelving, but I'm trying to ignore it. I just hate that someone might be led astray by that. I almost couldn't bring myself to read this because of that. But one of my close GR friends rated it so highly and I trust her judgment that I couldn't turn it down; and I'm so happy I pushed myself.<br /><br />For the record, I do believe many readers are confused by "chick lit" and "women's literature" or "fiction by women writers". It doesn't help that so many are marketed so freaking insanely in order to garner readers.<br /><br />What I'm saying is that this is a book that I think even male readers could appreciate. And I worry that the label "chick lit" could be a real turn-off for most.<br /><br />I can't give this a full 5 stars because it really consists of more dialogue than I prefer. I often find that an easy way out; Gavalda, however, manages to throw in an occasional paragraph or few pages that are not as superficial as straight conversation, and for that alone I hung on.<br /><br />Now, really. Can Gavalda write a book just about Camille? For me? Please and thank you.<br /><blockquote>All the valves opened and she blew her nose against his shirt, cried some more, letting go of twenty-seven years of solitude, of sorrow, of nasty blows to the head, crying for the cuddles she never had, her mother's madness, the paramedics on their knees on the wall-to-wall carpet, her father's absent gazes, the shit she went through, all those years without any respite, ever, the cold, the pleasure of hunger, the wrong paths taken, the self-imposed betrayals, and always that vertigo, the vertigo at the edge of the abyss and of the bottle. And the doubt, her body always in hiding, and the taste of ether and the fear of never being good enough.</blockquote><br /><br />That, right there. That's one of those sections that hit me somewhere I didn't know existed. You don't have to understand. That one is just for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18170297077499142927" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1767438334" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1767438334" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900147-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544395177p2/900147.jpg" /></a>

This is the tale of four downtrodden individuals who are thrown together by fate or random chance, whatever you want to call it, and the unlikely friendships that develop (keeping the rags with the napkins, one might say).<br/><br/>This book makes me want to move to Paris and find three quirky roommates of my own.<br/><br/>There are many contenders, but my favorite quote from</span>
  <span id="freeText4551677808982553082" style="display:none">This book will break your heart and then mend it and repeat the cycle. All I wanted to do was read it all day - I didn't want to put it down. This is the tale of four downtrodden individuals who are thrown together by fate or random chance, whatever you want to call it, and the unlikely friendships that develop (keeping the rags with the napkins, one might say).<br /><br />This book makes me want to move to Paris and find three quirky roommates of my own.<br /><br />There are many contenders, but my favorite quote from this book:<br /><br /><blockquote>…Regarding intellectuals … It’s easy to knock them. Really easy. They’re usually not very muscular and they don’t put up a good fight. It doesn’t turn them on – the sound of marching boots, or medals, or big limos – so, no, it’s not hard to take them down. All you have to do is rip the book from their hands, or the guitar, or the pencil, or the camera, and instantly they turn into useless, hopeless oafs. As a matter of fact, that’s usually the first thing a dictator does: break their eyeglasses, burn their books or ban their concerts. It doesn’t cost him much, and it can help him avoid all sorts of bother further down the line. But, you see, if being an intellectual means you like to learn, that you’re curious and attentive and can admire things and be moved by them and try to understand how it all hangs together, and try to go to bed a bit less stupid than the day before, well, then, yes: not only am I an intellectual but I’m proud to be one. Really proud, even.</blockquote></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer404238817423240918">I don't mind books that are essentially character studies. The things is, to enjoy them I have to like the characters. <br/>Ok, I did like 3 out of 4 of the main characters... I loved, loved, <i>loved</i> Philibert. What can I say? I have a weakness for stuttering history buffs, apparently. I liked Paulette and Camille (most of the time) but I just couldn't care about Franck. He was really a jerk.   <span id="freeText404238817423240918" style="display:none">I don't mind books that are essentially character studies. The things is, to enjoy them I have to like the characters. <br />Ok, I did like 3 out of 4 of the main characters... I loved, loved, <i>loved</i> Philibert. What can I say? I have a weakness for stuttering history buffs, apparently. I liked Paulette and Camille (most of the time) but I just couldn't care about Franck. He was really a jerk. His foul mouth got on my nerves too. The first word in every sentence doesn't have to be an expletive, Franck dear! And since after the first 150 pages or so it was mainly his and Camille's story I had to force myself to keep reading as the middle dragged on.<br />The translation wasn't great either. It was rather hard to follow at times and a little awkward to read.<br />But I still liked it. If nothing else, it gave me a new literary crush. I wish Philibert could get his own book, hehe.</span>

<div id="review_446713598" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/446713598" />
    <a title="Josefine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9749960-josefine"><img alt="Josefine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1337876604p2/9749960.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/446713598?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josefine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josefine" href="/user/show/9749960-josefine">Josefine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9749960-josefine?shelf=translated">translated</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer446713598" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2845453414480789418">I started reading this with a certain amount of apprehension due to it's overwhelming popularity (anything widely and universally acclaimed usually makes me suspicious) and because of the obvious feel-good connotations. Despite this the first few chapters had me hooked, and I started to think that the book might actually be as wonderful as everyone claims. The characters are nuanced and flawed enough to feel believable (the language issues that inevitably mar all translated work are evident here</span>
  <span id="freeText2845453414480789418" style="display:none">I started reading this with a certain amount of apprehension due to it's overwhelming popularity (anything widely and universally acclaimed usually makes me suspicious) and because of the obvious feel-good connotations. Despite this the first few chapters had me hooked, and I started to think that the book might actually be as wonderful as everyone claims. The characters are nuanced and flawed enough to feel believable (the language issues that inevitably mar all translated work are evident here as well, but acceptable), and the story hints at enough darkness to keep it interesting. I wanted to find out more about all the people in the book, their backgrounds and connections, what had happened to them and where they were going. What is Camille's tragic background? Who will Franck become? Who exactly are Paulette and, most mysterious of all, Philibert? Which is why the second half is so disappointing. The unfolding love story is just too predictable and cliché, the individual angst of the various characters isn't explored in enough depth, and Philibert (who appears to be going through the most interesting development of all during this time) disappears from the story almost completely, only to make occasional cameos. The finale has the story tied up in a bow so neat that a part of me desperately wanted one final devastating catastrophe to make its way into the last paragraph and blow the feel-good apart. <br /><br />I would have given the first half four stars, because the first half is very good. The second half (other than the syrupy epilogue) is not bad but simply very run-of-the-mill. It's a shame, because with a bit more daring and risktaking I feel this book really could have lived up to the hype - and been no less feel-good, albeit in a slightly less conventional way.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2845453414480789418" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16391824179982862814">This book was recommended to me by a friend. She really loves it and spoke of it very warmly, so when she brought it back for me to borrow and read I couldn't say no. <br/><br/>It is a very sweet story of four very different, but equally lost lives intertwining and impacting each other. There's also a bit of romance, but it's the good kind.   <span id="freeText16391824179982862814" style="display:none">This book was recommended to me by a friend. She really loves it and spoke of it very warmly, so when she brought it back for me to borrow and read I couldn't say no. <br /><br />It is a very sweet story of four very different, but equally lost lives intertwining and impacting each other. There's also a bit of romance, but it's the good kind. In fact, this book is a wonderful look into the kinds of relationships that exist in the world - friendship, family, romance, be it what it may. <br /><br />It starts out really bleak, with characters that seem trivial and not particularly likeable, but give it time, you'll learn to love them as they evolve and get a chance to shine a bit. <br /><br />It's just such a precious story. Not great literature, but it's kind, it's warm, it's intelligent and it's interesting. I'm glad my friend persisted. </span>

          </span>

    <a title="abcdefg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1492096-abcdefg"><img alt="abcdefg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451868476p2/1492096.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/58338421?book_show_action=true">Jun 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="abcdefg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="abcdefg" href="/user/show/1492096-abcdefg">abcdefg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1492096-abcdefg?shelf=spl">spl</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1492096-abcdefg?shelf=400-500">400-500</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer58338421" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4927954125245509607">I really enjoyed this book until about two-thirds of the way into it when it started to get predictable and I could easily picture it being made into a romantic comedy film (which it was made into in France, unfortunately). <br/><br/>Don't get me wrong, I'm not a hater of romance or comedy or even romantic comedies. I just don't like it when they're predictable because it's formulaic and not very challenging to me as a story.<br/><br/>It starts off with Camille, who I really did sympathize with as this waif genius </span>
  <span id="freeText4927954125245509607" style="display:none">I really enjoyed this book until about two-thirds of the way into it when it started to get predictable and I could easily picture it being made into a romantic comedy film (which it was made into in France, unfortunately). <br /><br />Don't get me wrong, I'm not a hater of romance or comedy or even romantic comedies. I just don't like it when they're predictable because it's formulaic and not very challenging to me as a story.<br /><br />It starts off with Camille, who I really did sympathize with as this waif genius artist who really is all alone in the world with nothing better than a dead-end job and a nowhere life as far as anyone's concerned. But then she's rescued by an eccentric intellectual, starts to open the heart of an angry chef, and begins to take care of his ailing grandmother and I felt convinced at a certain point that this was all very touching.<br /><br />Unfortunately, there's a point towards the last portion of the novel where she pisses me off. I won't say what it is because I don't want to give it away, but let's just say someone special to her in the novel opens up and she can't even say a few lousy words. It just really irritated me. Her excuse of course was that she was "scared". <br /><br />I would have ditched her. It's like, how bad do you want it? Let's see. But she didn't even prove herself when it came to that test. So I was ultimately dissatisfied with that result. Nonetheless, as far as romantic comedies go, guess who lives happily ever after? You got it.<br /><br />So, 3 out of 5 for me. 3 for a good start and a relatively sweet book. <br /><br />Another thing: the writing was sparse in places to an extent where there are pages of dialogue and you can't really tell at times who's speaking because conceivably, one or the other character could be saying what's being said. That, to me, didn't really reflect good technique as far as writing is concerned. This novel could have been filled out a bit more in that one respect.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4927954125245509607" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_164066031" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/164066031" />
    <a title="Mary Ronan Drew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2986995-mary-ronan-drew"><img alt="Mary Ronan Drew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1302365296p2/2986995.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/164066031?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary Ronan Drew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary Ronan Drew" href="/user/show/2986995-mary-ronan-drew">Mary Ronan Drew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer164066031" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15200275646518744479">How do you say chick lit in French? This charming novel by Anna Gavalda has all the characteristics we want in a love story, with a good bit of French sophistication superimposed. It’s translated from the French by Alison Anderson, and I mention that because it is so smoothly done it doesn’t feel translated.<br/><br/>When I finished reading the book I looked on Amazon to see if there might possibly be a sequel, though the book ends so well and pleasingly that a sequel isn’t really possible with the same c</span>
  <span id="freeText15200275646518744479" style="display:none">How do you say chick lit in French? This charming novel by Anna Gavalda has all the characteristics we want in a love story, with a good bit of French sophistication superimposed. It’s translated from the French by Alison Anderson, and I mention that because it is so smoothly done it doesn’t feel translated.<br /><br />When I finished reading the book I looked on Amazon to see if there might possibly be a sequel, though the book ends so well and pleasingly that a sequel isn’t really possible with the same characters. I wanted to spend more time with them. I wanted to know what happened to them next. In other words, they were much more three-dimensional than most characters in a book that can be classified as a romance.<br /><br />The structure of the book is clever. The grandmother of an eccentric young aristocrat named Philibert has died and until the estate is settled he is assigned by his family to live in her 4,000 sq ft apartment on the Champs de Mars so that none of the squabbling cousins can take it over. He expects a letter from the lawyers any day saying things have been settled and he must move out.<br /><br />Meanwhile he has a roommate, Franck, a chef with tremendous skill with food but not with people. His language is profane and his girlfriends are many and short-term. But he and Philibert get along well enough as they have worked out a system where they seldom see one another. But both are nervous about change, so when Philibert rescues a young woman, Camille, who has been living in a tiny garret in their building, things become unsettled.<br /><br />A talented artist who dropped out of the Ecole de Beaux Arts to live with a man with half her own talent, Camille has drifted, living on the street for a time. She is now working for a professional cleaning company who vacuum and tidy office in the middle of the night. Her co-workers, though not important to the story, are interesting, especially a woman from Senegal with a personality as big as her person.<br /><br />Camille and Franck don’t get along and Franck’s depression at having put his grandmother, who lives in Tours, in a nursing home and the stress of his spending every day off driving to and back and spending the day with her wear on all of them. When the grandmother moves into the apartment with them Camille and Philibert fall in love with her and for a time things go well. But their comfortable situation is not to last and some tough decisions have to be made.<br /><br />For a short time in my youth I lived on the rue Bonaparte very near the Ecole de Beaux Arts and walked the streets mentioned in the book. Camille loves the view in all directions from the middle of the Pont des Arts, a footbridge across the Seine, the bridge I took whenever I went to the other bank of the river. It was a delight to share this and other bits of Paris with Camille.<br /><br />While I was at Amazon.com searching in vain for a sequel I sent a copy of this book to my sister, who is a pastry chef, and who I know will love the many descriptions of a Parisian restaurant kitchen, the people who work there, and the cooking they do – always under tremendous pressure. Franck it turns out is a superb cook and is the kitchen’s saucier (no 3 man in an old-fashioned brigade-style kitchen, immediately under the chef and sous-chef.)<br />A couple of other comments: I can’t remember who told me about this author and this book, but I’m grateful. Thank you. I thought about buying a copy for my Kindle until I realized the Kindle version is $13.99 and the paperback version, with its beautiful cover (a tray of chalks) is $11.56. Do save $2.50 and get the trade paperback.<br /><br />2011 No 69 Coming soon: Moneyball<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15200275646518744479" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_489549284" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/489549284" />
    <a title="Stuti (Turmeric isn&#39;t your friend. It will fly your ship" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6372822-stuti-turmeric-isn-t-your-friend-it-will-fly-your-ship"><img alt="Stuti (Turmeric isn&#39;t your friend. It will fly your ship" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1458489431p2/6372822.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/489549284?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuti (Turmeric isn&#39;t your friend. It will fly your ship" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuti (Turmeric isn&#39;t your friend. It will fly your ship" href="/user/show/6372822-stuti-turmeric-isn-t-your-friend-it-will-fly-your-ship">Stuti (Turmeric isn&#39;t your friend. It will fly your ship</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Stuti by: </span>
      the content sighs of discontent people across the world and in my house
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6372822-stuti-turmeric-isn-t-your-friend-it-will-fly-your-ship?shelf=realistic-fiction">realistic-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer489549284" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1711279754885682918"><b>3.5</b><br/><br/>When it was good, it was marvelous and when it was bad, it was supremely boring.<br/><br/>Here's how it went-<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380402638i/782747._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I think I should backtrack a bit. When it was bad, it was supremely boring and eye-roll-inducing <i>and</i> happy.<br/><br/>It's amazing how a book that starts off with such depressing characters in such deep shits can end so happily and hoppy-go-lucky-ily. With any other book, it would have been uncouth and it would not compute. But in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47780.Hunting_and_Gathering' title='Hunting and Gathering by Anna Gavalda' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hunting and Gathering</a>, it fits just perfectly. <br/><br/>No plot, no story, just plain</span>
  <span id="freeText1711279754885682918" style="display:none"><b>3.5</b><br><br>When it was good, it was marvelous and when it was bad, it was supremely boring.<br><br>Here's how it went-<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380402638i/782747._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I think I should backtrack a bit. When it was bad, it was supremely boring and eye-roll-inducing <i>and</i> happy.<br><br>It's amazing how a book that starts off with such depressing characters in such deep shits can end so happily and hoppy-go-lucky-ily. With any other book, it would have been uncouth and it would not compute. But in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47780.Hunting_and_Gathering" title="Hunting and Gathering by Anna Gavalda" rel="nofollow noopener">Hunting and Gathering</a>, it fits just perfectly. <br><br>No plot, no story, just plain character watching is <i>not</i> my kinda book. And it is everything that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47780.Hunting_and_Gathering" title="Hunting and Gathering by Anna Gavalda" rel="nofollow noopener">Hunting and Gathering</a> is. Totally character-driven. Which is even more amazing because I didn't even like the characters, especially in the beginning. <br><br>There's Camille Facaque, whose blatant use of words like 'slut' for secretaries and the occasional holier-than-thou attitude is very unbecoming in a protagonist. But she grew on me, like creases on my books, in the sense that they both become endearing sorts after awhile.<br><br>Then we have Frank Lestafier, whose blatant use of sensitive terminology(oh and add this to Camille's list too) like 'rape' and the general she's-holier-than-thou attitude and off-hand treatment of previous girlfriends is very unbecoming in the male lead. But he's still likable from the beginning, with the way he always put up a brave front and all. <br><br>Also, we have many side characters: the whining old woman, Paulette, whom I liked somewhat when we read from her POV but otherwise, not so much. The never-be-a-professor Philibert, and Yvonne, Paulette's long term friend and caretaker whom I absolutely loved, precisely because she doesn't play a major role in the book.<br><br>The thing that grabbed me some ways into the book was the way entire scenes were conveyed in the way of dialogues, with not a word in the otherwise. There were also some three-page monologues which would have been great had the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/26826.Anna_Gavalda" title="Anna Gavalda" rel="nofollow noopener">Anna Gavalda</a> made them the characteristic of one particular character, let's say, Franck. But when every character in a hundred-page radius gives such long speeches, the reiteration not only becomes tiresome but also confusing.<br><br>That said, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/26826.Anna_Gavalda" title="Anna Gavalda" rel="nofollow noopener">Anna Gavalda</a> incorporates <i>some</i> emotions, folks. Had I lesser ice in my veins and my heart not so picky, I'd have been crying to shambles whilst reading this book. And I adore the way she writes. I really do. It's very sublime<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ she uses a lot of that word. Well, the french version <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br><br>The ending is abrupt and like I mentioned earlier and opportune and jovial. In fact, I hated it when I finished it earlier on my way to school, but those were just early morning jitters. For now, I've made my peace with it and I do like the last paragraph of the epilogue, much as it cramps my style.<br><br>Overall, the meh's did not outweigh the pros nor did the opposit occur. And hence, the average rating. I would not <i>not</i> recommend it to anyone but neither would I go out of my way to recommend it to anyone. There are much more awesome books, IMO.<br><br><a href="http://attheendofthestory.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">Cross-posted on Books behind Dam{n}s</a></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="1711279754885682918" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_587964249" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/587964249" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1788674-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1432902126p2/1788674.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/587964249?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/1788674-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782290343715" href="/book/show/21416065-ensemble-c-est-tout">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1788674-jill?shelf=fran%C3%A7ais">français</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer587964249" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17954545123961666535">Anna Gavalda has a wonderfully infuriating way of writing like so...<br/>Making your eyes flitter down the page.<br/>Making your heart bat harder with each revelation.<br/>Until she concludes a thought.<br/>Like this.<br/>With oh-so-much meaning.<br/><br/>Her writing is more alive than most, an especially impressive feat since I've read her work in French, not English, and I lose myself and die a thousand readerly deaths much more often when reading in French. But her words flow inevitably forward. If, as many authors have procla</span>
  <span id="freeText17954545123961666535" style="display:none">Anna Gavalda has a wonderfully infuriating way of writing like so...<br />Making your eyes flitter down the page.<br />Making your heart bat harder with each revelation.<br />Until she concludes a thought.<br />Like this.<br />With oh-so-much meaning.<br /><br />Her writing is more alive than most, an especially impressive feat since I've read her work in French, not English, and I lose myself and die a thousand readerly deaths much more often when reading in French. But her words flow inevitably forward. If, as many authors have proclaimed, the goal of a writer is to get the reader to read the next sentence, and the next, and the next, Anna Gavalda is nothing short of a genius. Myself and others may be tempted to label this short, direct style as cutesy and simplistic, but it's gosh darn compelling, if we're being frank.<br /><br />Less compelling, however, is the story behind the words. We have a nearly plotless novel here; all that happens is outlined in the blurb, apparent from the initial chapters, and fated by the gods of storytelling. Nothing much occurs and what does occur does not surprise. The four characters--a ragtag team beaten by the world who will find happiness in their shared ruins and slowly rebuild each other--are terrific sketches, but what they do is nowhere near as passionate as Gavalda's prose. I've only previously read Gavalda's slam-dunk short story collection, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47775.Je_voudrais_que_quelqu_un_m_attende_quelque_part" title="Je voudrais que quelqu'un m'attende quelque part by Anna Gavalda" rel="nofollow noopener">Je voudrais que quelqu'un m'attende quelque part</a>, which hides this weakness of hers. Petite novelettes are perfect for her character-centric writing; this novel of nearly 600 pages was vast and vacant, the winds of disinterest blew through its empty and tired intrigues.<br /><br />And yet.<br />That writing.<br />Heavy on punctuation, on labelling feelings, on fragmenting thoughts.<br />It pulled me through. <br /><br />(I'll stop now.)<br /><br />But this pathetic attempt at imitation shows how much her words tumbled and scattered in my brain. Gavalda might not tell the stories that I want to read, but she tells them in the way I want to read them. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17954545123961666535" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_32583977" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/32583977" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/32583977?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782290006146" href="/book/show/2408092.Hunting_and_gathering">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=philo-psychol">philo-psychol</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=france">france</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer32583977" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10633486610170174979">And finally the third volume "Je voudrais que quel'un m'attende quelque part" is completed. This volume I liked least. It is not that the writing is lacking, but rather that I do not like short stories. The first story was best - "Petites Pratiques Germanopratines Mobile". This one I liked alot - it is for all those who really hate mobiles..... I totally agreed with the main character. I should make a rule for myself - never, absolutely never read short stories. They are not my thing! I didn't c</span>
  <span id="freeText10633486610170174979" style="display:none">And finally the third volume "Je voudrais que quel'un m'attende quelque part" is completed. This volume I liked least. It is not that the writing is lacking, but rather that I do not like short stories. The first story was best - "Petites Pratiques Germanopratines Mobile". This one I liked alot - it is for all those who really hate mobiles..... I totally agreed with the main character. I should make a rule for myself - never, absolutely never read short stories. They are not my thing! I didn't completely finish the volume b/c I just had to start reading another book that was awaiting on the shelf. I have chosen to keep the 4 stars because it is just me that dislikes short stories, and the other novels I enjoyed alot! Ensemble C'Est Tout was the best, in my opinion.....<br /><br />OK now I have finished "Je l'aimais", translated as "I loved him"! WOW, this author is VERY good at depicting what makes people tick and also clearly saying what should guide people's actions. I happen to agree with her. I will read the short stories in "Je voudrais que quelqu'un m'attend" later. I have already seen the movie "Ensemble, c'est tout". The movie was great. I wonder if I will like the short stories - usually I don't like short stories. I will definitely give them a try. I usually do not like books that point out how one should live one's life - nevertheless I did like this book! A good writer can make you like everything even that you didn't think you liked. And it was very enjoyabe reading French! When I read the short stories I will mark the book again as "currently reading". I want to savour this awhile before I continue.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10633486610170174979" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_7757233" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7757233" />
    <a title="Catie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/49416-catie"><img alt="Catie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1175707232p2/49416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/7757233?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catie" href="/user/show/49416-catie">Catie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer7757233" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17177320833191786526">Every lovely thing that I have read about Anna Gavalda is true. This book is lovely, heartbreaking, and so very hopeful without resorting to sentiment and a flowery vocabulary; I can't say enough good things about it. <br/><br/>Gavalda's style is at once minimalistic and incredibly colorful and provocative. She plunges her reader into the middle of conversations, then sets the scene as she goes rather than vice versa. Some may find that dissorienting, but I found that it greatly improved my ability to rel</span>
  <span id="freeText17177320833191786526" style="display:none">Every lovely thing that I have read about Anna Gavalda is true. This book is lovely, heartbreaking, and so very hopeful without resorting to sentiment and a flowery vocabulary; I can't say enough good things about it. <br /><br />Gavalda's style is at once minimalistic and incredibly colorful and provocative. She plunges her reader into the middle of conversations, then sets the scene as she goes rather than vice versa. Some may find that dissorienting, but I found that it greatly improved my ability to relate to, and see from the characters' points of view. At the end of the novel I didn't feel that I was friends with Philou, Camille, Franck, and Paulette, so much as that I had watched them and loved them their whole lives. They were real, vital, <i>breathing</i> people. For various reasons I connected Camille with Sarah Poley's character, Hanna, in <i>The Secret Life of Words</i>. Though their stories are very different, their pasts are a mystery to us for much of their stories and they both need healing. <br /><br />My one regret is that I read a translation of the novel. My French is no where near good enough to make it through a 480+ page novel. I'm just glad that my pitiful knowledge of the language was enough to understand some of the cultural humor(particularly the "tu" and "vous" jokes).<br /><br />This little book gripped me from the first chapter and held my interest. I can't wait to explore the rest of Gavalda's writing. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17177320833191786526" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_35264892" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/35264892" />
    <a title="Trena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1253638-trena"><img alt="Trena" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/35264892?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trena" href="/user/show/1253638-trena">Trena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9782290006221" href="/book/show/1273348.Ensemble_c_est_tout">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Trena by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/1435703-karen">Karen</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer35264892" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11131225285180650002">This book took me quite a while to get into, but eventually I got really hooked. The story is slow to start and it's hard to care about the characters at first, but they do grow on you and by the end you're really invested in them. The style was also something that took me a while to appreciate; perhaps it's a French way of storytelling. The style seems similar to the movie Amelie with an isolated yet charming misfit who manages to find other misfits and eventually opens up (and in fact a google</span>
  <span id="freeText11131225285180650002" style="display:none">This book took me quite a while to get into, but eventually I got really hooked. The story is slow to start and it's hard to care about the characters at first, but they do grow on you and by the end you're really invested in them. The style was also something that took me a while to appreciate; perhaps it's a French way of storytelling. The style seems similar to the movie Amelie with an isolated yet charming misfit who manages to find other misfits and eventually opens up (and in fact a google search shows that a movie was made of this book starring Audrey Tatou). I read the first 30 or 40 pages at first, then a few more pages, and then the last 350 or so in two marathon sessions.<br /><br />The translation is pretty dreadful, in some ways. This is not a book that is going to appeal to masses of American readers, and yet the translator Americanized some things (such as calling welfare "AFDC") in ways that are confusing and unnecessary, as though American readers could not possibly understand that the French welfare system has a different name. The translation of the title is also extremely random. How "Together, This is Everything" becomes "Hunting and Gathering" I haven't the faintest notion. I wish there was an edition translated the way they do poems, with the translation on one page and the original on the facing page. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11131225285180650002" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('xei5jnNidUGYXa6/XkpvW1uVNqLBiskLpKD5t86P3Rv/ztBcyDy8KT92Gt6pv2WEjcrRag8HR2z/3r8GA8wvgQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('70EUck/QOr0B8XLJYrfe8oMP7uioamCMw0hWVTzAfLDVZ32g9I7z1abaxqiVQtQtVVAJIGbn7uuYNhDk8YOOKg==')})
  }

Pas étonnant, donc, que son premier roman, Soutien-gorge rose et veston noir, soit fortement inspiré du climat urbain branché, pétillant et osé qui a fait le succès de cette émission.  L\'histoire est découpée en épisodes qui relatent une année dans la vie tumultueuse de Chloé Saint-Mars. Recherchiste pigiste, la jeune femme de 28 ans est surtout irrémédiablement célibataire, ayant relégué aux oubliettes l\'amour des contes de fées auquel elle croyait à 10 ans. Avec Juliette et Antoine, ses inséparables alter ego, elle a rédigé le Manifeste du célibat qui clame :la vie plate des gens casés, c\'est pas pour nous autres. Bien sûr, l\'amour pointera son nez, et Chloé devra réviser ses positions. En dépit de longueurs et d\'inutiles répétitions, ce roman est le portrait réussi d\'une jeunesse privilégiée et bourrée de contradictions. Les filles ne croient pas à l\'amour, mais passent leur temps à en parler. Ça baise beaucoup, ça boit encore plus, ça parle cru, mais ça rêve de mariage en blanc, de bébés et d\'amour éternel, un amour encore vif, patiné et enrichi. Rafaële Germain, qui a aussi 28 ans et dont les chroniques dans La Presse lui ont déjà acquis un grand nombre de lecteurs, prend le pouls de sa génération avec empathie, humour et une bonne dose de sagesse. --Monique Roy<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"1575766127110210784\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div She is the daughter of a tedious parliamentarian, a talented and startlingly lucid child who has decided to end her life on the sixteenth of June, her thirteenth birthday. Until then she will continue behaving as everyone expects her to behave: a mediocre pre-teen high on adolescent subculture, a good but not an outstanding student, an obedient if obstinate daughter. \n\nPaloma and Renée hide both their true talents and their finest qualities from a world they suspect cannot or will not appreciate them. They discover their kindred souls when a wealthy Japanese man named Ozu arrives in the building. Only he is able to gain Paloma\'s trust and to see through Renée\'s timeworn disguise to the secret that haunts her. Doch nun wirft Michael Kumpfmüller ein helles, fast heiteres Licht auf den berühmten Dichter und zeichnet liebevoll und diskret einen Menschen, der in seinem letzten Jahr die große Liebe findet und sein Leben in die Hand nimmt, bevor es dafür zu spät ist. Im Sommer 1923 lernt der tuberkulosekranke Franz Kafka, als Dichter nur Eingeweihten bekannt, in einem Ostseebad die 25-jährige Köchin Dora Diamant kennen. Und innerhalb weniger Wochen tut er, was er nicht für möglich gehalten hat: Er entscheidet sich für das Zusammenleben mit einer Frau, teilt Tisch und Bett mit Dora. In Berlin wagt er mit ihr das gemeinsame Leben, mitten in der Hyperinflation der Weimarer Republik. Den täglich kletternden Preisen, den wechselnden Untermietquartieren, den argwöhnischen Eltern zum Trotz: Bis zu seinem Tod im Juni 1924 werden sich Franz Kafka und Dora Diamant, von wenigen Tagen abgesehen, nicht mehr trennen. Aus dieser wahren Geschichte macht Michael Kumpfmüller einen feinsinnigen, behutsamen und kenntnisreichen Liebesroman. Kafkas Tagebücher, seine Briefe und letzten Texte kennt er genau und webt sie zart in die Erzählung ein. Aber ebenso sehr widmet er sich Doras Sicht, dem Blick der verliebten jungen Frau auf ihren rätselhaften, sterbenden Mann. Und so gelingt Kumpfmüller eine tief anrührende Parabel über das Leben und die Liebe, das Schreiben und den Tod.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"308144546851798943\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/26826.Anna_Gavalda">About Anna Gavalda</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/26826.Anna_Gavalda"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1236883498p3/26826.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16157415114910080406">Anna Gavalda is a French teacher and award-winning novelist.<br/><br/>Referred to by Voici magazine as "a distant descendant of Dorothy Parker", Anna Gavalda was born in an upper-class suburb of Paris. While working as French teacher in high school, a collection of her short stories was first published in 1999 under the title "Je voudrais que quelqu'un m'attende quelque part" that met with both critical acc</span>
  <span id="freeText16157415114910080406" style="display:none">Anna Gavalda is a French teacher and award-winning novelist.<br /><br />Referred to by Voici magazine as "a distant descendant of Dorothy Parker", Anna Gavalda was born in an upper-class suburb of Paris. While working as French teacher in high school, a collection of her short stories was first published in 1999 under the title "Je voudrais que quelqu'un m'attende quelque part" that met with both critical acclaim and commercial success, selling more than three-quarters of a million copies in her native France and winning the 2000 "Grand Prix RTL-Lire." The book was translated into numerous languages including in English and sold in twenty-seven countries. It was published to acclaim in North America in 2003 as "I Wish Someone Were Waiting for Me Somewhere." The book received much praise and is a library and school selection worldwide in several languages.<br /><br />Gavalda's first novel, Je l'aimais (Someone I Loved) was published in France in February 2002 and later that year in English. Inspired by the failure of her own marriage, it too was a major literary success and a bestseller and was followed by the short (96 pages) juvenile novel 35 kilos d'espoir (95 Pounds of Hope) that she said she wrote "to pay tribute to those of my students who were dunces in school but otherwise fantastic people".<br /><br />In 2004, her third novel, "Ensemble c'est tout," focused on the lives of four people living in an apartment house: a struggling young artist who works as an office cleaner at night, a young aristocrat misfit, a cook, and an elderly grandmother. The 600-page book is a bestseller in France and has been translated into English as Hunting and Gathering.<br /><br />As of 2007, her three books have sold more than 3 million copies in France. Ensemble c'est tout was made into a successful movie in 2007 by Claude Berri, with Audrey Tautou and Guillaume Canet. The adaptation of her first novel, Je l'aimais, with Daniel Auteuil and Marie-Josée Croze, was filmed in 2009 by Zabou Breitman.<br /><br />Divorced, and the mother of two, Gavalda lives in the city of Melun, Seine-et-Marne, about 50 km southeast of Paris. In addition to writing novels, she also contributes to Elle magazine.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16157415114910080406" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
